    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/337303831" />
    <a title="Allison" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7825668-allison"><img alt="Allison" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1338233997p2/7825668.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/337303831?book_show_action=true">May 26, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Allison" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Allison" href="/user/show/7825668-allison">Allison</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099505969" href="/book/show/5973826-the-redeemer">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer337303831" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7801683955391688079">562 pages of brilliance. <br/><br/>The English-speaking publication industry has destroyed Jo Nesbo's fantastic Harry Hole (pronounced "Hula" like Hula Hoop) series, not only by omitting the first two books in this series by starting it on the third book (<i>The Redbreast</i>), but also by switching the order of a few books in the series. Whatever the case, make sure you are reading these in order: they are brilliant. Did I say that already?<br/><br/>It's understandable then why I had a hard time finding this book. Not on</span>
  <span id="freeText7801683955391688079" style="display:none">562 pages of brilliance. <br /><br />The English-speaking publication industry has destroyed Jo Nesbo's fantastic Harry Hole (pronounced "Hula" like Hula Hoop) series, not only by omitting the first two books in this series by starting it on the third book (<i>The Redbreast</i>), but also by switching the order of a few books in the series. Whatever the case, make sure you are reading these in order: they are brilliant. Did I say that already?<br /><br />It's understandable then why I had a hard time finding this book. Not only is this book the <i>only</i> one missing from this series at my local library, but it is also missing from the libraries in nearby counties as well as in e-book downloads. In the end, I had to purchase this used and wait for it to arrive. Even then, I was hesitate about starting the novel simply because <u>The Devil's Star</u> blew my mind and I was convinced that nothing could top it. <br /><br />I was, fortunately, mistaken. <br /><br />If you are not a fan of the series, become one. I was glad that this book was so thick since I didn't want to put it down (and I didn't - I finished it within 24 hours). I'm too excited and can't wait to start on the next one. It is easy to say that Jo Nesbo is the master of plot, the MC of words, and the ringleader of exceptional narrative. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7801683955391688079" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3427339-jeffrey-keeten?shelf=nordic-noir">nordic-noir</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2119669657" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17764760341334830344"><b>‚ÄùAnd for one vulnerable moment Harry felt nothing but sympathy. Not the sympathy he could feel for the victim or for the next of kin, but for the person who for one heartrending moment sees his own pathetic humanity.‚Äù</b><br/><br/>Harry Hole has looked in the mirror many times and seen the stark pitiful vision of his own existence. His own human frailty too real to bear, but there is always a new case to keep him from drowning in despair.<br/><br/>Something smells fishy in the ranks of the Salvation Army in Oslo, Norwa</span>
  <span id="freeText17764760341334830344" style="display:none"><b>‚ÄùAnd for one vulnerable moment Harry felt nothing but sympathy. Not the sympathy he could feel for the victim or for the next of kin, but for the person who for one heartrending moment sees his own pathetic humanity.‚Äù</b><br /><br />Harry Hole has looked in the mirror many times and seen the stark pitiful vision of his own existence. His own human frailty too real to bear, but there is always a new case to keep him from drowning in despair.<br /><br />Something smells fishy in the ranks of the Salvation Army in Oslo, Norway, and it isn‚Äôt just the odor coming from the investigating officer‚Äôs tennis shoes. <br /><br /><i>‚Äù‚ÄôYou should get yourself a couple of new insoles for the sneakers you‚Äôve got in there,‚Äô she said, pointing.<br /><br />He eyed her in astonishment.<br /><br />‚ÄòYou don‚Äôt have to be Jean-Baptiste Grenouille to recognize the smell,‚Äô she added.<br /><br />‚ÄòPatrick Suskind,‚Äô he said. <b>‚ÄòPerfume.‚Äô</b><br /><br />‚ÄòA policeman who reads,‚Äô she said.<br /><br />‚ÄòA Salvation Army soldier who reads about murder,‚Äô he said. ‚ÄòWhich leads us back to the reason for my being here, I‚Äôm afraid.‚Äô‚Äù</i><br /><br />An attractive woman in a snappy, Salvation Army uniform who references the main character of the cult classic <i>Perfume</i> would turn the head of any man of discerning taste, but for a lonely man like Harry Hole, it is like seeing an unexpected blue haze of water in the middle of the Sahara desert. He is intrigued, maybe even a bit besotted. I‚Äôd chastise Harry because he is in love with another woman and barely hanging onto a few months of sobriety, but I was right there with him, wanting to keep this woman talking to see what other interesting literary allusions might fall from her pretty lips. <br /><br />Martine.<br /><br />And so young. <br /><br />Harry has a new boss who keeps a cast on his desk of the pinky finger of a fanatical, Japanese officer from WW2 who cut off his finger when his superior did not allow him to counterattack. <br /><br />The pinky says it all. <br /><br />This is going to be a difficult working relationship. <br /><br />Harry has a lack of social survivability skills. He says what he means without a filter. He pushes things to the breaking point when he should let it go. He likes being alone, or so he says, but really he is just still searching for the person who will complete him. The person who will make him want to stay sober. He sees things and makes connections that others do not make. He is the best detective in the department, and if he weren‚Äôt, he‚Äôd have already been bounced out of the department, and we would be reading about Harry the Truck Driver or Harry the Bouncer. <br /><br />He has caught an interesting case involving the very public, very professional shooting of a mid-level officer in the Salvation Army. Who would want to kill someone in God‚Äôs army? <br /><br />Harry soon finds himself in a desperate chase that has him running through the streets of Oslo, trying to catch up with the killer who is called <b>The Little Redeemer.</b> The case has him meeting with the mother of a Serbian, resistance fighter to trade a life for a life. He finds himself searching through empty, shipping containers on the docks and is nearly eaten by a rare, but vicious Metzner guard dog. There are junkies who know seemingly insignificant pieces of the plot. The twists and turns of the changing truth would leave most investigators‚Äô minds corkscrewed into a babbling mess of incoherent suppositions. Harry‚Äôs mind just continues to refine what he knows, sets aside what is confusing, until finally the facts become incontestable.<br /><br />And the new partner assigned to Harry learns very quickly to just let him work and not to try to keep up with the jumps in logic. Sometimes, Harry leaps Grand Canyons. Who wants to flail and fall through the long darkness to only find Harry‚Äôs painful grin waiting for you at the bottom so he can elucidate for you who and why? Drive him where he wants driven. Do what he asks and enjoy the front row view of not only the reveal of the killer, but also of the mastermind behind it all. <br /><br />Oh, and Tom Waaler, from <i>The Devil‚Äôs Star</i>, the series entry before this one, is a phantom continuing to lurk on the edges of every Harry inspired success. Some things are just never put to bed. <br /><br />As always Jo Nesbo delivers an exciting thriller that scratches that Nordic Noir itch I get at least once a month. Next for me is <i>Snowman</i> to be properly prepared to watch Michael Fassbender metamorphose into Harry Hole in the movie release on October 20th, 2017. <br /><br />If you wish to see more of my most recent book and movie reviews, visit<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.jeffreykeeten.com">http://www.jeffreykeeten.com</a><br />I also have a Facebook blogger page at:<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.facebook.com/JeffreyKeeten">https://www.facebook.com/JeffreyKeeten</a></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13626334956559395355">3.5 stars<br/><br/>I know what to expect from Nesb√∏, but I keep going back for more hoping it gets at least slightly better. At times, I thought the book was great. At other times I could not wait for it to end. That is how every Nesb√∏ book I read so far is, and I wish it wasn't! <br/><br/>At the beginning of the book, he starts off with a scene I understand and then follows it up by jumping around to a bunch of seemingly unrelated scenes. Now, you might say, "but, Matthew, that is what a mystery is and the fun is </span>
  <span id="freeText13626334956559395355" style="display:none">3.5 stars<br /><br />I know what to expect from Nesb√∏, but I keep going back for more hoping it gets at least slightly better. At times, I thought the book was great. At other times I could not wait for it to end. That is how every Nesb√∏ book I read so far is, and I wish it wasn't! <br /><br />At the beginning of the book, he starts off with a scene I understand and then follows it up by jumping around to a bunch of seemingly unrelated scenes. Now, you might say, "but, Matthew, that is what a mystery is and the fun is in the journey bringing them together!" I agree wholeheartedly! However, Nesb√∏ is really good at muddying the waters to the point of distraction before things become clear. Just when I think I am getting what's happening, a scene starts and I am all like, "who is this, where are we, and how does this relate at all". I am hoping some of you out there understand what I am saying about good confusion vs. bad confusion.<br /><br />I will say that one of the best parts of this book was the ending. So, it is worth all of the confusion to get there.<br /><br />Side note: I got a chance to read this whole book in under 24 hours on a business road trip for work. As a road trip book, I give it 6 out of 10 stars because a road book should keep me engaged and awake. The confusing jumping around was causing me to lose interest and I kept checking how much time was left.</span>

  <span id="freeText14288968702509594759" style="display:none"><br>In this 6th book in the 'Harry Hole' series, the detective is on the trail of a wily hitman. The book can be read as a standalone.<br><br>*****<br><br>As the story opens, it's 1991 and the 14-year-old daughter of a high official in the Norwegian Salvation Army is raped by someone she knows. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1591542174i/29609709._SX540_.jpg" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>This apparently goes unreported as there seem to be no consequences. During that same year - during the break-up of Yugoslavia - a Croatian youth is dubbed "Little Redeemer" for his brave deeds against Serbian militias. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1591542174i/29609710.png" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Fast forward a dozen years and the "Little Redeemer" - now a hitman for hire - goes to Norway and kills Robert Karlsen, a respected young member of the Norwegian Salvation Army. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1591542174i/29609711.jpg" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The hitman soon discovers he mistakenly assassinated the wrong guy. He then goes after Robert's brother Jon Karlsen, the true target. Enter Inspector Harry Hole and his team of detectives, whose job it is to capture the murderer and prevent further deaths. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1591542174i/29609712.jpg" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>A great deal of the book involves the hitman chasing Jon Karlsen and Hole's team chasing the killer. <br><br>Much happens along the way: the killer demonstrates clever skills in escaping the cops and hiding out; Hole travels to Croatia; a police officer is stabbed; a woman is murdered in a horrifying way; another girl is raped, and much more.<br><br>Of course there are plenty of side issues: Robert Karlsen has a reputation for liking young girls and seems to have gotten friendly with a 15-year-old Croatian refugee. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1591542174i/29609713.jpg" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Jon Karlsen has a girlfriend but is also involved with a married woman whose husband wants to purchase Salvation Army properties. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1591542174i/29609715.jpg" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Hole develops a relationship with the pretty Salvation Army worker, Martine, who was raped at the beginning of the book. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1591542174i/29609716.jpg" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>And there are lots more interesting characters who interact in a variety of ways.<br><br>Jo Nesbo loves to include lots of misdirection and unexpected twists in his books, and he outdoes himself here. The book is chock full of surprises. This is a complex, engaging story. A good mystery thriller.<br><br>You can follow my reviews at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://reviewsbybarbsaffer.blogspot.com/">https://reviewsbybarbsaffer.blogspot....</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="14288968702509594759" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_868961400" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/868961400" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4086118-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567611029p2/4086118.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/868961400?book_show_action=true">Mar 01, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/4086118-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780307595850" href="/book/show/13131706-the-redeemer">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Michael by: </span>
      Harry Roolaart
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=norway">norway</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=religion">religion</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=police-procedural">police-procedural</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer868961400" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer775146941484398491">This is a great step up from a mystery serving just as entertainment. Though some might quibble over whether Nesbo should be shelved with ‚Äúliterature‚Äù, I felt like I was treated to a serious tour of complex social issues. As pointed out so well by Harry Roolaart here on Goodreads, the new genre of Nordic Noir can often tagged as ‚Äúnatural realism‚Äù with its coverage of the failing side of the progressive dream of these counties to become an egalitarian paradise . Here the topics include the proble</span>
  <span id="freeText775146941484398491" style="display:none">This is a great step up from a mystery serving just as entertainment. Though some might quibble over whether Nesbo should be shelved with ‚Äúliterature‚Äù, I felt like I was treated to a serious tour of complex social issues. As pointed out so well by Harry Roolaart here on Goodreads, the new genre of Nordic Noir can often tagged as ‚Äúnatural realism‚Äù with its coverage of the failing side of the progressive dream of these counties to become an egalitarian paradise . Here the topics include the problems of drug addiction and prostitution and the more hidden crimes among certain leading members of the society, in this case the vaunted Salvation Army. As usual with crime fiction, we become invested with the mission of the detective to serve as a proxy physician to address the ills of society. In this case, our hero Harry Hole has to deal with whether he is willing to assume the role of ‚Äúredeemer‚Äù, which by one definition is ‚Äúa person who brings goodness, honor, etc., to something again.‚Äù<br /><br />The book starts with the rape of a 14-year old girl at a Salvation Army summer camp. Not grossly wrought, just a mysterious background to the events in the story 12 years later. We are put into the mind of an assassin who executes a member of the Salvation Army on a public street in Oslo. We are soon given the knowledge that he is known as ‚ÄúThe Little Redeemer‚Äù for his role as a boy soldier who blew up Serbian/Yugoslavian tanks in the siege of Vukovar in Croatia in the 1991 war. Harry Hole is working on the death of a heroin addict when the case comes up, which involves him already familiarizing himself with the wonderful work of the Salvation Army in serving these folks. Much of the novel covers his slow and brilliant efforts to solving both the whodunit, that the reader already knows, and the whydunnit, which is a deep mystery. As it soon becomes clear that the hitman is not done and has another target, the pressures mount, terrible mistakes are made, and the stress and guilt Harry feels leads him to struggle with staying on the wagon with respect to his drinking problem.<br /><br />The Christian concept of redemption under Protestant schemes is a relatively passive affair of accepting Christ as the son of God who experienced human death to atone for our sins. But in Nesbo‚Äôs book the theme of redeemer is of a more active and violent means of achieving divine justice, as if to redeem is a transitive verb. At one point one character poses the question: ‚ÄúIf God doesn‚Äôt do His job, though, someone has to do it.‚Äù The epigram at the start of the book is from Isaiah which speaks of the Messiah in warrior terms: ‚ÄúWho is this that comes from Edom, coming from Bozrah, his garments stained with crimson? Who is this, in glorious apparel, marching in the greatness of his strength? ‚ÄòIt is I, who announce that right has won the day, it is I,‚Äô says the Lord, ‚Äòfor I am Mighty to save.‚Äô ‚Äú The Salvation Army is not known for operating in this retributive mode, although I have always been perplexed by the martial metaphor in the hymn ‚ÄúOnward Christian soldiers, marching as to war ‚Ä¶‚Äù; I suppose that dates back to their temperance activities in the 19th century. This book is no indictment of the mission of the Salvation Army, which does great humanitarian work in 126 countries (Wikipedia tells me that in the U.S. alone they help over 32 million people a year with a budget over $3 billion). However, the tale does delve into how an upbringing in a Salvation Army family can twist some members‚Äô personalities. <br /><br />The plot is fascinating enough, but I got more pleasure from Harry‚Äôs character development. I am at some disadvantage reading this out of series order, but it is clear that Hole is more isolated than usual due to his role in a violent solution of a case of pervasive police corruption in the prior book. Yet at one point as he is getting close a woman in the Salvation Army community, she gets him to admit that he has always been lonely and a loner. I am still simmering over his self-assessment on that:<br /><i>‚ÄúBjarne Moller, my former boss, says people like me always choose the line of most resistance. It‚Äôs in what he calls our ‚Äòaccursed nature.‚Äô That‚Äôs why we always end up on our own. I don‚Äôt know. I like being alone. Perhaps I have grown to like my self-image of being a loner, too. ‚Ä¶‚Äù</i><br /><br />Another character puts her finger on the true challenges Harry faces in negotiating the moral gray zones of the modern world he inhabits:<br /><i>You‚Äôve discovered that guilt is not as black-and-white as you thought when you decided to become a policeman and redeem humankind from evil. As a rule there‚Äôs little evil but a lot of human frailty. Many sad stories you can recognize in yourself. However, as you say, one has to live. So we start lying. To those around us and to ourselves.</i><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="775146941484398491" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1133169939" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1133169939" />
    <a title="Matt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5903843-matt"><img alt="Matt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603562140p2/5903843.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1133169939?book_show_action=true">Dec 16, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt" href="/user/show/5903843-matt">Matt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099505969" href="/book/show/5973826-the-redeemer">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1133169939" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2047871284348524702">In the sixth novel from the Harry Hole series, Nesb√∏ adds a slight religious flavour to the story, with strong symbolism throughout. When Robert Karlsen, a member of the Salvation Army, is gunned down during a public concert, the identity of the shooter remains a mystery, baffling the authorities. Harry Hole leads the investigation, though there is little on which to act, except a description that includes a red neckerchief. Unbeknownst to them, a Croatian soldier-cum-hitman, Stankic, was been p</span>
  <span id="freeText2047871284348524702" style="display:none">In the sixth novel from the Harry Hole series, Nesb√∏ adds a slight religious flavour to the story, with strong symbolism throughout. When Robert Karlsen, a member of the Salvation Army, is gunned down during a public concert, the identity of the shooter remains a mystery, baffling the authorities. Harry Hole leads the investigation, though there is little on which to act, except a description that includes a red neckerchief. Unbeknownst to them, a Croatian soldier-cum-hitman, Stankic, was been paid for the shooting, but soon realises that he struck the wrong Karlsen. Rather than leaving town, Stankic doubles back and tries to kill Jon Karlsen to set things right. It is at this point that Hole begins to learn a little more and almost captures the elusive Stankic. Heading to Croatia to tie up some loose ends in the form of clues, Hole realises that Stankic is being handled by his mother. Hole makes a promise to ensure the authorities do not kill her son if Hole is told who ordered the hit. All is revealed to Hole, as well as a substantial motive. Returning to Norway, Hole must track down not only the killer but those who have been committing other heinous crimes within the Salvation Army and bring it all to an end before there are more casualties. However, Hole suffers a devastating event of his own, which focusses his attention to solve the case and get justice for all. In a way that only Hole can do, all cases converge and lead to a suspense-filled finale where there will be blood, but whose? Nesb√∏ fascinates his readers yet again with this explosive tale.<br /><br />The Hole series finds new and impressive ways to get better with each story. I find myself enthralled the more I read and cannot rest when Hole is on the prowl. Told from a more complex and darker perspective than North American thrillers, Nesb√∏ thickens this book with significant character development (as he has in all the other novels) and inserts powerful story arcs that punish the reader for skimming or skipping books in the series. Nothing is more refreshing than seeing an author use all their skills to weave a book of much worth together as the breadcrumbs lead to an ultimate crime that only the master storyteller could construct. Jo Nesb√∏ knows how to tell a story and does so with such ease!<br /><br />Kudos Mr. Nesb√∏ for another gem. Your ideas are ceaseless, which makes me want to keep reading.<br /><br />Like/hate the review? An ever-growing collection of others appears at: <br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://pecheyponderings.wordpress.com/">http://pecheyponderings.wordpress.com/</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="2047871284348524702" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Lyn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn"><img alt="Lyn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495541962p2/5253785.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2116306200?book_show_action=true">Sep 06, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lyn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lyn" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn">Lyn</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2116306200" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15638016131880152819">Jo Nesbo anticipates and writes an early, Harry Hole version of his brilliant 2014 novel <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/19101283.The_Son' title='The Son by Jo Nesb√∏' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Son</a>.<br/><br/>First published in Norwegian in 2005 as Frelseren, and then translated to English in 2007, The Redeemer blends elements of fundamentalist Christianity with street violence, drug culture and sex crimes.<br/><br/>The Salvation Army is structured as a war agent against sin and so this view from the front lines makes sense to some degree, but the discontinuity between the two extremes was at times unsettling. In t</span>
  <span id="freeText15638016131880152819" style="display:none">Jo Nesbo anticipates and writes an early, Harry Hole version of his brilliant 2014 novel <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/19101283.The_Son" title="The Son by Jo Nesb√∏" rel="nofollow noopener">The Son</a>.<br><br>First published in Norwegian in 2005 as Frelseren, and then translated to English in 2007, The Redeemer blends elements of fundamentalist Christianity with street violence, drug culture and sex crimes.<br><br>The Salvation Army is structured as a war agent against sin and so this view from the front lines makes sense to some degree, but the discontinuity between the two extremes was at times unsettling. In the capable hands of a talented writer like Nesbo, though, the dichotomy is explored and mined for all its worth, and we see roles reversed and twists and turns revealed in an exciting and fast moving thriller.<br><br>Hole and his dysfunctional Oslo detectives are on the trail of an execution in broad daylight of a Salvation Army soldier when the trail gets deep into international crime and hidden secrets. Nesbo‚Äôs dynamic portrayal of the Oslo crime squad continues to entertain.<br><br>For Hole fans and for Nordic noir aficionados.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1504746740i/23810560._SY540_.jpg" width="400" height="400" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>

<div id="review_1359854425" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1359854425" />
    <a title="Phrynne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8655084-phrynne"><img alt="Phrynne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1577753809p2/8655084.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1359854425?book_show_action=true">Aug 10, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Phrynne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Phrynne" href="/user/show/8655084-phrynne">Phrynne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781846550409" href="/book/show/5973825-the-redeemer">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1359854425" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2674671050979713423">Number six in the Harry Hole series and another fantastic book from <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/904719.Jo_Nesb_' title='Jo Nesb√∏' rel='nofollow noopener'>Jo Nesb√∏</a>. I struggled a bit at the beginning as the POV switched from one person to another with no explanation as to who each person was. However it soon became clear that there was a good reason for this and it certainly helped to prolong the sense of mystery. Harry is his usual self, smarter than the average cop, always refusing to bow down to authority and in a constant battle with alcoholism. The story is gripping and it is</span>
  <span id="freeText2674671050979713423" style="display:none">Number six in the Harry Hole series and another fantastic book from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/904719.Jo_Nesb_" title="Jo Nesb√∏" rel="nofollow noopener">Jo Nesb√∏</a>. I struggled a bit at the beginning as the POV switched from one person to another with no explanation as to who each person was. However it soon became clear that there was a good reason for this and it certainly helped to prolong the sense of mystery. Harry is his usual self, smarter than the average cop, always refusing to bow down to authority and in a constant battle with alcoholism. The story is gripping and it is almost impossible to guess what will happen next.I found it to be a really great read and would recommend it to anyone who enjoys a good, gritty police thriller.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_559291753" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/559291753" />
    <a title="Harry" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7597429-harry"><img alt="Harry" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1380595404p2/7597429.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/559291753?book_show_action=true">Mar 12, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Harry" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Harry" href="/user/show/7597429-harry">Harry</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099505969" href="/book/show/5973826-the-redeemer">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7597429-harry?shelf=detective-mystery">detective-mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7597429-harry?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7597429-harry?shelf=nordic-crime-fiction">nordic-crime-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer559291753" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15676238284383222428">Here's the thing about the recent popularity of Scandinavian writers and if you're a Nordic Thriller aficionado you couldn't care less about the distinction: the novels are depressed, somber, filled with ennui, a lack of humor, with flawed characters if not suffused with a strong tendency towards determinism; in short, whether you're reading <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/706255.Stieg_Larsson' title='Stieg Larsson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Stieg Larsson</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/22339.Henning_Mankell' title='Henning Mankell' rel='nofollow noopener'>Henning Mankell</a>, or <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=Jo%20Nesbo' title='Jo Nesbo' rel='nofollow noopener'>Jo Nesbo</a> you are likely reading <a href='http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Naturalism_(literature)' rel='nofollow noopener'>Literary Naturalism</a>. If you live in Scandinavia you might consider this par for the cours</span>
  <span id="freeText15676238284383222428" style="display:none">Here's the thing about the recent popularity of Scandinavian writers and if you're a Nordic Thriller aficionado you couldn't care less about the distinction: the novels are depressed, somber, filled with ennui, a lack of humor, with flawed characters if not suffused with a strong tendency towards determinism; in short, whether you're reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/706255.Stieg_Larsson" title="Stieg Larsson" rel="nofollow noopener">Stieg Larsson</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/22339.Henning_Mankell" title="Henning Mankell" rel="nofollow noopener">Henning Mankell</a>, or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=Jo%20Nesbo" title="Jo Nesbo" rel="nofollow noopener">Jo Nesbo</a> you are likely reading <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Naturalism_(literature)" rel="nofollow noopener">Literary Naturalism</a>. If you live in Scandinavia you might consider this par for the course, ennui is imbued into the populace (as it is also reflected in the works of prominent Russian writers - <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15823480.Anna_Karenina" title="Anna Karenina by Leo Tolstoy" rel="nofollow noopener">Anna Karenina</a> comes to mind). Just as we continue to struggle here in the States with our history of slavery and the resulting racial tensions, so do Europe and Scandinavia struggle in coming to terms with Nazism and the Bolshevik revolution (More than a few reviewers have expressed their dissatisfaction with the Nordic writers' pre-occupation with Nazism). And yet, the rise in popularity of these Nordic thrillers here in the States is puzzling given our strong tendency towards literary Romanticism. We like for the good guys to win, we like emotion, we like our heroes (as opposed to anti-heroes) we enjoy free will, and in general consider ourselves in control of our own lives.<br /><br />Having said that: there is excellence in Literary Naturalism. The above doesn't mean we can't enjoy a well written novel, an intriguing mystery, a flawed anti-hero, a well crafted story written in the style of literary Naturalism. It doesn't mean we can't enjoy the works of <a href="http://jonesbo.com/#!/about-the-author" rel="nofollow noopener">Jo Nesbo</a>. I did.<br /><br />In Jo Nesbo's words: <i>"I come from a family of readers and story tellers."</i> With a librarian mother and a father who sat before the fire and told the kids stories they wanted to hear (each repetition bringing something new to the tale) Jo's foundation was carved in stone. Again, in his own life story we sense the determinism filtering into his life: he wanted to be a soccer star but an injury put a quick stop to this; with a dreadful feeling of fate guiding his life he entered the military in the hopes something would happen (what happened was "Self-Discipline"); thinking he might want to be an economist he entered the world of finance which he abandoned as well; someone told him he could play guitar (he only knew 3 chords) and he formed several bands, <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?feature=endscreen&amp;v=rvDtBgH5wHM&amp;NR=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Di Derre</a> being the most successful; and finally he wrote (on an airplane to begin with) and he never stopped.<br /><br /><i>The Redbreast</i> is Jo Nesbo's third Harry Hole (pronounced "Hooleh") novel (the other two not being translated for a US audience as of yet) and is Nesbo's claim to fame. So, this is where we start. Yes, the books should be read in order! For an American audience, Harry Hole can be likened to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/search?utf8=%E2%9C%93&amp;query=Harry+Bosch" rel="nofollow noopener">Harry Bosch</a>; he defies authority, is an outcast within his own organization, is best left alone to do this job (his office is at the end of the hall), is more of an anti-hero than a hero, has trouble with his romantic life, lives alone, has a fierce propensity for justice (as opposed to the Law) and once let loose is like a pit bull with a bone fastened to his jaws. But perhaps the most compelling reason why Harry Hole has such a following is Nesbo's devastating characterization of what exactly comprises a flawed hero. Upon reflection, American hard-boiled writers don't come close to accomplishing the same. This is not too dissimilar to the way Nesbo sees himself.<br /><br /><i>Bjarne M√∏ller, my former boss, says people like me always choose the line of most resistance. It's in what he calls our 'accursed nature'. That's why we always end up on our own. I don't know. I like being alone. Perhaps I have grown to like my self-image of being a loner, too....I think you have to find something about yourself that you like in order to survive. Some people say being alone is unsociable and selfish. But you're independent and you don't drag others down with you, if that's the way you're heading. Many people are afraid of being alone. But it made me feel strong, free and invulnerable.</i><br /><br />And...ah, yes, there is the matter of plot! So how do we justify this decided streak of fate/determinism within the novels with Nesbo's apparent mastery of plot? The two seemingly ought to contradict each other. On the one hand, we have Nesbo's almost Shakespearean tendency to cast characters as marionette puppets on the strings of fate (the very opposite of plot), while on the other hand we are riveted by the very complex actions and reactions made by Harry Hole during his investigations (Nesbo is a master at not adding anything superfluous to his novels). Perhaps it is an unholy marriage between the two that transfixes us. His plots are intricate, very complex, the seemingly irrelevant details exposed throughout the novels become larger than life as the story closes, and they can weave through time, forward and backward, as the story unfolds. But, with a little alacrity, we can remember we are reading Naturalism and so it isn't always Harry Hole making events happen, but rather the reverse, it is the events that move Harry Hole. Again, it is a matter of preference but in Nesbo's case it is done with utter expertise as a writer.<br /><br />The exposition/setting is often Scandinavia: the weather is somber, the descriptions grey-like, the people absorbed with alcohol and withdrawn, if not bundled and sequestered. And yet, the dialogue and scenes are full of references to other millieus', continents, languages, and cleverly hidden philosophical references that speak to a widely cultured audience (as opposed to American writers of this genre who rarely venture beyond the borders of their land, if not their own State). And as with plot, there are no superfluous details. Everything in the novels matters and Nesbo does not forget even the tiniest detail to which he's made a seemingly furtive reference earlier on in the story. This is one of the biggest reasons why I love Jo Nesbo.<br /><br />I thoroughly enjoyed Jo Nesbo's <i>The Redbreast</i> and am currently reading the remaining Harry Hole novels. I remain intrigued by events left undone (such as the fate of our undiscovered villain in this and other stories). You'll just have to read the novels to find out more.<br /><br />Oh, yes, as with other series this review is likely to be repeated for all (unless there is a drastic divergence from what I have written here). So, if you've read this review, you've read 'em all. Enjoy!</span>
  <a data-text-id="15676238284383222428" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer406443880597337250">This was reasonably good. Great twist and revelation at the end. But it does go on and on. Too many sub-plots and flashbacks. Too many suspects. Just like in <b>The Snowman</b>, there are instances where the case is thought to be solved but then there are more revelations and twists. I wonder whether this is Nesbo's formula. I have only read a couple of books by him so far. <br/><br/>The salvation army setting was interesting. I liked the way the book began with the account of life in a salvation army camp from </span>
  <span id="freeText406443880597337250" style="display:none">This was reasonably good. Great twist and revelation at the end. But it does go on and on. Too many sub-plots and flashbacks. Too many suspects. Just like in <b>The Snowman</b>, there are instances where the case is thought to be solved but then there are more revelations and twists. I wonder whether this is Nesbo's formula. I have only read a couple of books by him so far. <br /><br />The salvation army setting was interesting. I liked the way the book began with the account of life in a salvation army camp from the point of view of a girl who is waking up to her sexuality. The life of the assassin on the run was also quite entertaining. I was not satisfied with the police procedural aspects of the novel. Nobody does that like Thomas Harris. <br /><br />I'm done with Nesbo for now. I might check out more of his work in the future.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_667333608" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/667333608" />
    <a title="Cathy DuPont" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6637209-cathy-dupont"><img alt="Cathy DuPont" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1393680963p2/6637209.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/667333608?book_show_action=true">Jul 14, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cathy DuPont" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cathy DuPont" href="/user/show/6637209-cathy-dupont">Cathy DuPont</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780307595850" href="/book/show/13131706-the-redeemer">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Cathy by: </span>
      Ed Yorke and Harry Roolaart
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer667333608" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12392965825456931309"><b>4.5 Stars Rounded up to Five</b><br/><br/>Harry Roolaart, my Goodreads friend Harry, said Jo Nesbo is a must read for me and he was right. However, I didn't enjoy Harry Hole (pronounced, per Harry R. as Hoo-leh) near as much as "our Harry." But then, I'm not sure anyone loves and enjoys Nesbo's writing and his character Harry Hole, as much as "our Harry." (Harry Roolaart is from the Netherlands and he IS cosmopolitan. I'm not. I'm southern.)<br/><br/>Yes, Harry, yes, I enjoyed this book and enjoyed it a lot. As a matte</span>
  <span id="freeText12392965825456931309" style="display:none"><b>4.5 Stars Rounded up to Five</b><br /><br />Harry Roolaart, my Goodreads friend Harry, said Jo Nesbo is a must read for me and he was right. However, I didn't enjoy Harry Hole (pronounced, per Harry R. as Hoo-leh) near as much as "our Harry." But then, I'm not sure anyone loves and enjoys Nesbo's writing and his character Harry Hole, as much as "our Harry." (Harry Roolaart is from the Netherlands and he IS cosmopolitan. I'm not. I'm southern.)<br /><br />Yes, Harry, yes, I enjoyed this book and enjoyed it a lot. As a matter of fact, I enjoyed it more than the first one I read, <i>The Redbreast</i>. <br /><br />With that said, "our Harry" wrote an excellent review and I agreed with all he said excepting these few sentences:<br /><br /><i>But perhaps the most compelling reason why Harry Hole has such a following is Nesbo's devastating characterization of what exactly comprises a flawed hero. Upon reflection, <b>American hard-boiled writers don't come close to accomplishing the same.</b> This is not too dissimilar to the way Nesbo sees himself.</i> (My boldface.)<br /><br />Many American readers may disagree with that comment. I do. The first "flawed hero" who comes to my mind is Dave Robicheaux although as readers, we know he is married and less a loner than Harry Hole; another comes to mind, Michael Connelly's Harry Bosch. <br /><br />I'm sure others have their own suggested "flawed heroes" who were created by American authors and may disagree with Harry's statement. <br /><br />Other than that, please take a look at Harry's review...it's thoughtful and articulate and you can read the admiration Harry has for Jo Nesbo and his character, Harry Hole. Harry's review of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/559291753" rel="nofollow noopener">The Redeemer</a> is here.<br /><br />My one complaint, and it's purely my personal problem, is the Norwegian names. They drive me crazy and yes, I know they can't be Tom, Dick and <i>(oops, sorry, Harry)</i> Harry. But from the names in Nesbo's books, I can't tell if the person introduced is a man or woman. So I'm reading along, and in my mind it's "he this" and "he that" then, come to find out, it's a woman! Crap...have to go back and re-read it with a a woman in mind. <br /><br />Take a look at these names and see if <b>YOU</b> can absolutely, positively tell if they are a man or a woman. And no, I don't have the answer. <br /> <br />(I'm not by myself either on this. Reading some reviews of <i>The Redbreast</i> some time ago, a fellow was complaining about the names as well and asked "what's with those tiny zeros above some letters? And the slash through the "o's"...what's with that?" Poor guy has a point...what's with that? Damned if I know.) <br /><br />Here we go....J√∏rgen Juve (George Juvie?); Ole Solskj√¶r (Helloooo and one strange letter(s) of the alphabet combining a&amp;e? huh?); Tore Andr√© Flo (feminine last name, I think); Stine Bredal Oftedal (what?); P√•l Grotnes (Pal with tiny "o's" above the "a" and last name Grotnies?); Jan √Öge Fj√∏rtoft (sounds feminine to me); Odd Iversen (sounds odd to me); Marit Malm Frafjord (a woman?); Olav Nilsen (clueless); Ase Birkrem (how to pronounce that, ass?); √òyvind Leonhardsen (huh?)...get my drift? <br /><br />No. Up front, no, I am not cosmopolitan needless to say. But I'm trying. Never mind, I've given up already. I speak southern. But will read another Nesbo. Strange names and all...it was a fantastic book. <br /><br />Thanks, Ed...thanks, Harry.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12392965825456931309" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_819362005" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/819362005" />
    <a title="Mal Warwick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4739424-mal-warwick"><img alt="Mal Warwick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1294014688p2/4739424.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/819362005?book_show_action=true">Jan 10, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mal Warwick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mal Warwick" href="/user/show/4739424-mal-warwick">Mal Warwick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099505969" href="/book/show/5973826-the-redeemer">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4739424-mal-warwick?shelf=mysteries-thrillers">mysteries-thrillers</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer819362005" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer175161162580365173">It‚Äôs inevitable. Mystery stories are always grounded in some level of manipulation by the author. A virtual army of murder suspects mysteriously comes into focus in the background, one at a time. Trusted figures reveal hidden motives. The author deliberately conceals some crucial element by simply truncating a scene before the reader can discern what‚Äôs happening. All this is the stuff of the traditional parlor mystery and locked-room puzzles parodied in the board game Clue (Colonel Mustard was f</span>
  <span id="freeText175161162580365173" style="display:none">It‚Äôs inevitable. Mystery stories are always grounded in some level of manipulation by the author. A virtual army of murder suspects mysteriously comes into focus in the background, one at a time. Trusted figures reveal hidden motives. The author deliberately conceals some crucial element by simply truncating a scene before the reader can discern what‚Äôs happening. All this is the stuff of the traditional parlor mystery and locked-room puzzles parodied in the board game Clue (Colonel Mustard was found with Mr. Boddy in the Library, but Professor Plum did it with the Candlestick in the Dining Room).<br /><br />In a well-crafted mystery, outright manipulation of this sort is kept to a discreet minimum, so that the reader doesn‚Äôt feel badly used. Jo Nesbo, whom I consider one of the best mystery writers alive, normally manages to drive his stories forward without creating a sense that I‚Äôm being duped. He failed the test in The Redeemer, the sixth of his ten novels to date featuring Inspector Harry Hole of the Oslo police.<br /><br />Despite all the early signs of a disturbing and credible Harry Hole tale, The Redeemer gradually unveils an awkward dependency on all the traditional tools of manipulation employed by so many of Nesbo‚Äôs forebears ‚Äî and all too many of his contemporaries. The story becomes virtually unwieldy, with seemingly unrelated murder mysteries intersecting in suspect ways and too many characters turning out to be dramatically different from the ways they were first portrayed. To make matters worse, the theme behind the book‚Äôs title comes to light in the first pages and is repeated throughout in heavy-handed ways.<br /><br />However, with all this said, Nesbo‚Äôs skill in building suspense, and my abiding interest in the profoundly complex character of Harry Hole, The Redeemer kept my interest to the end.<br /><br />In recent years I‚Äôve reviewed The Redbreast, Nemesis, and The Leopard, all of which I found to be brilliant, as well as The Bat, the first of Nesbo‚Äôs Harry Hole series, which was promising but clumsy.</span>
  <a data-text-id="175161162580365173" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_545861677" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/545861677" />
    <a title="Marc Nash" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5136053-marc-nash"><img alt="Marc Nash" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442559977p2/5136053.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/545861677?book_show_action=true">Feb 25, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marc Nash" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marc Nash" href="/user/show/5136053-marc-nash">Marc Nash</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099505969" href="/book/show/5973826-the-redeemer">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer545861677" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12235308200068066250">This is my 3rd Nesbo book in the Harry Hole detective series. And I've had diminishing returns with each read. Maybe I was unlucky to have started off at the apex with "The Snowman". Perhaps it's unfortunate that having read the most recent two, now I am having to go backwards in the series which may not best serve up his work. Or perhaps it's because I have a problem with character-led series. I've said it in my review of "The Leopard", but what new insight into Hole or any other main character</span>
  <span id="freeText12235308200068066250" style="display:none">This is my 3rd Nesbo book in the Harry Hole detective series. And I've had diminishing returns with each read. Maybe I was unlucky to have started off at the apex with "The Snowman". Perhaps it's unfortunate that having read the most recent two, now I am having to go backwards in the series which may not best serve up his work. Or perhaps it's because I have a problem with character-led series. I've said it in my review of "The Leopard", but what new insight into Hole or any other main character can the author reveal by book ten without me the reader thinking why didn't that trait reveal itself before? <br /><br />But that wasn't necessarily the problem with this book, although how many more times can Hole face death at the hands of the killer/ save someone from the same, or sleep with someone who turns out to be psychologically damaged beyond good health? Actually, there's considerably less of the first two in this than the others I've read. In fact there's tranches of text where not very much happens at all, apart from characters being en route to somewhere else. The bodycount is low in this book. There's a lot about the Salvation Army's history and internal politics within Norway which was dull and maybe of more significant to Norwegian life than it is to say Britain's. Instead you get these strange energy flows within most chapters, whereby the crescendo end of the chapter has to do the work of picking up the flagging momentum that precedes it; but Nesbo has this recurring device of ending a chapter with someone being given a revelation in a bag or by phone message, but the reader doesn't get to see what it is. It just irritates me as a reader rather than playing with my emotions or interest, because it is so constantly contrived.<br /><br />As to the third of the incredulous strands, that of Hole getting the girl, well the episode in this novel is rather distasteful to my sensibilities. A woman who was raped at fourteen (which is the start of the book), has kept herself pure out of a mixture of her Salvation Army faith and her own trauma of the original rape. And yet along comes our 'Arry, reformed alcoholic (sort of) and sweeps her off her feet, irrespective of the fact she is tangled up in his current investigation. I don't know, I find it a touch troubling that a woman who has avoided sex after being raped, should finally be prepared to cast off her inhibitions because of Hole. I think that represents a bit of moral blindness by the author, since Hole is so identified with Nesbo and Hole is a man who can apparently 'cure' traumatised rape victims of their fear of sex and perhaps also turn homosexuals straight...<br /><br />The reason i didn't give this book 1 star, was that actually there's rather a good character portrayal in it. A refugee from the bloody Yugoslavian conflict is drawn in a fully-fledged and sympathetic fashion. Unlike the other bad guys in this who are just bad (in all senses of the word) and homeless junkies who populate the book as the background service users of the Salvation Army who are all thinly drawn. I was genuinely engaged by the story and fate of the Yugoslav refugee and for that reason alone I gave the book its second star. But I don't anticipate reading any more Harry Hole adventures. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12235308200068066250" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2532814340" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5195964723987848399">Nesbo Delivered yet again! A change of leadership at the Politi and a hired gun (workaholic!) assassin will still not stop Harry Hole from arriving at the truth. Add to it the Christmas spirit and religious symbolism - forgiveness, redemption and suffering and Nesbo delivers a cracker of mystery that is both fast paced and intelligent.<br/><br/>It all starts with a rape. Fast forward to the present where a Salvation Army member is murdered in public and Harry with his team leads the investigation. In a pa</span>
  <span id="freeText5195964723987848399" style="display:none">Nesbo Delivered yet again! A change of leadership at the Politi and a hired gun (workaholic!) assassin will still not stop Harry Hole from arriving at the truth. Add to it the Christmas spirit and religious symbolism - forgiveness, redemption and suffering and Nesbo delivers a cracker of mystery that is both fast paced and intelligent.<br /><br />It all starts with a rape. Fast forward to the present where a Salvation Army member is murdered in public and Harry with his team leads the investigation. In a parallel track, we find the assassin who is called the little redeemer (whose values are shocking for a killer!), realizing he killed the wrong person and is determined to fulfill the contract. What unfolds is a sequence of twists and turns and clever red herrings placed along the way. <br /><br />The second half of the book could have been a bit tighter, but then it is only since the first half almost races away. I could guess the who around 70% and so it was just a wait to the end. The Serbian war stories were sufficiently creepy and the twists fun. I wish, they had connected it up somehow with the victim storyline. <br /><br />The non-core part of the new boss and exchanges and attempts to tame Harry was fun. We see a more mature Harry who is not just a lone wolf, who also decides to groom youngsters in his team. The series can hopefully get better now that we have put behind the recurring thread from Book 3 on the smuggled arms. <br /><br />Intelligent read!</span>
  <a data-text-id="5195964723987848399" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText8670604155415424558" style="display:none">Oooooh. Harry Hole, Oslo police inspector, has a new tricky, multi-faceted deadly mystery in the sixth novel in the Harry Hole detective series, ‚ÄòThe Redeemer‚Äô. <br /><br />At first he has some joy in a clean desk and a re-start on his life! A new boss with no built-up grudges against Harry, Gunnar Hagen, is eager to earn his star in heaven. AA meetings are Hole‚Äôs chance to begin again with a clean slate, now that his greatest unsolved case has been cleared up (previous books in the series). Without Rakel, his ex-girlfriend and Oleg, Rakel‚Äôs son, to steady him, will the fresh start keep Hole from falling off the wagon? Jack Halvorsen, Harry‚Äôs young partner, is a likable and earnest innocent, and Harry wants to set a good example for him. Maybe it will be enough. <br /><br />First, some definitions of the words the author uses as titles:<br /><br />Advent - the arrival of a notable person<br /><br />Redeemer - one who redeems, say the dictionaries (Does anyone else have a problem with definitions which supposedly describe a word with a form of itself without any other clues?)<br /><br />Redeems - to discharge or fulfill; or to make amends<br /><br />Crucifixion - the act of crucifying (another definition which is not informative of meaning, only word type) <br /><br />Crucifying - to treat with gross injustice; to nail to a cross<br /><br />Mercy - act of kindness or pity<br /><br /><br />These words are the titles of the several parts of the novel ‚ÄòThe Redeemer‚Äô. Non- or nominal Christians may not understand the titles. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[It may be the gentle reader is unfamiliar with Christian theology due to that few Christians practice what they preach (knowing only the sound-bite or cherry-picked versions of Christian faith), or worse, choose only to follow Old Testament commands instead of the New Testament which was supposed to replace the former.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />We all know the most vicious killers all over the world tend to be religious fundamentalists who have been raised with a lot of ‚Äòcorrective‚Äô beatings based on religious teachings, most often involving matters of sexuality, which often leads to damaged self-hating adults. Extremist and evangelical Christians - and Muslims - appear to prefer creating punitive murderous psychotics in their family rather than recognize various aspects of human sexuality exist. The other nasty social habit of mankind in creating murderous psychotics is war. So what would happen if an atheist Norwegian police officer, aware that justice and his bosses are often compromised by politics, pandering and pay-offs, do when faced with two monster killers created by the top two most soul-destroying institutions of all societies - religion and war?<br /><br />I‚Äôm not telling, except to say I have relatives who are officers in the Salvation Army, and I agree with Hole‚Äôs final decision. <br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Can anyone explain why the war killer liked to wear urinal cakes?  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10863470374670303593">The Redeemer is book six in the Harry Hole series by Jo Nesbo. Christmas is for celebration, and Oslo's Salvation Army Branch organise a concert on the streets of Oslo, and everyone was joining themselves until someone killed one of the singers. Detective Harry Hole caught the case. The readers of The Redeemer will go on a rollercoaster ride with Detective Harry Hole to find the killer. <br/><br/>The Redeemer is the first book I have read of Jo Nesbo, and I did enjoy reading it.   <span id="freeText10863470374670303593" style="display:none">The Redeemer is book six in the Harry Hole series by Jo Nesbo. Christmas is for celebration, and Oslo's Salvation Army Branch organise a concert on the streets of Oslo, and everyone was joining themselves until someone killed one of the singers. Detective Harry Hole caught the case. The readers of The Redeemer will go on a rollercoaster ride with Detective Harry Hole to find the killer. <br /><br />The Redeemer is the first book I have read of Jo Nesbo, and I did enjoy reading it. The translator Don Bartlett also did an excellent job of translating Jo Nesbo story from Norwegian to English. Sometimes I find books that are translator lose the original plot of the story. However, in The Redeemer, this did not happen. I loved Jo Nesbo portrayal of his characters especially Detective Hary Hole. I also became engaged with the story after reading the first chapter. The way Jo Nesbo puts the twists and turns in the plot throughout The Redeemer ensures you are glued to your chair.<br /><br />The readers of The Redeemer will learn about the Salvation Army in Norway. Also, the readers of The Redeemer will learn about law enforcement procedures in Norway. The Redeemer highlights that situations are not always what they seem on the surface.<br /><br />I recommend this book.<br /><br /><br /></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer2605884838759888037">Jo Nesb√∏ is fast becoming one of my favourite authors and Harry Hole one of favourite detective protagonists. The Redeemer is #6 in the Harry Hole thrillers and the fourth in the Oslo sequence. The Redeemer was edgy, fast paced, layered and complex and is probably my favourite of the 6 books that I have devoured. Nesb√∏ uses the notions of forgiveness and redemption throughout the book, woven through a pseudo religious theme involving the Salvation Army church. <br/><br/>Harry is a complex character, authe</span>
  <span id="freeText2605884838759888037" style="display:none">Jo Nesb√∏ is fast becoming one of my favourite authors and Harry Hole one of favourite detective protagonists. The Redeemer is #6 in the Harry Hole thrillers and the fourth in the Oslo sequence. The Redeemer was edgy, fast paced, layered and complex and is probably my favourite of the 6 books that I have devoured. Nesb√∏ uses the notions of forgiveness and redemption throughout the book, woven through a pseudo religious theme involving the Salvation Army church. <br /><br />Harry is a complex character, authentically himself, genuine, loyal, lonely, self sabotaging and isolated. It is a privilege as the reader to catch glimpses inside his head and to get a sense of the tensions that live there. Tensions from which none of us are exempt, and which Harry becomes the one with the lived experience that we can observe from our living room couch and ponder about how similar we all actually are. I think Harry sums it up when he ponders during his conversation with his former chief, Bjarne Moller, ‚ÄúAnd to imagine you are at the heart of what you think is justice and then suddenly lose all sense of direction and become the very thing you oppose‚Äù. P 561. Replace the word justice with whatever characteristic we value and we start to get a sense of the message.<br /><br />So, The Redeemer, takes us on a circular journey in many ways. The hunter becomes the hunted, the hunted becomes the hunter and we wind up on a discombobulated merry-go-round.<br /><br />I loved every minute of this one.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2605884838759888037" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
